        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>The move from Coors Field to McAfee Coliseum did not go smoothly for Holliday.  He was on pace to hit fewer than 20 homers and his batting average was below .300 for the first time since his rookie season in 2004. American League pitchers were having success with fastballs against Holliday.  Pitch Type Values gave him a 2.00 wFB/C in 2008, but with the A’s he was essentially neutral, with a 0.18 mark. Holliday got a new lease on life when he was traded to St. Louis. Back in the National League, he hit nearly as well as he ever did in Colorado. National League pitchers threw him fewer fastballs, but Holliday had far greater success, with a 2.45 wFB/C.  He had a .423 wOBA with St. Louis, which brought his total for the season up to .390, compared to the .418 mark he posted his final season with the Rockies.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>In 2006-07, Holliday hit 70 homers. In 2008-09, he combined for 49. Whether or not Holliday can get back to his elite status will depend upon if he can once again be a 35-homer hitter.  His HR/FB rates were around 20% in 2006-07.  Last year with Oakland it was 9.7%, but it then rebounded to 16.7% with St. Louis. While the rate was still lower, Holliday compensated by hitting more fly balls than ever before.  He had a 39.2 fly-ball rate, virtually the same in both the AL and NL.  Holliday did have an ultra-high .391 BABIP with the Cardinals, but it was canceled out by the .319 mark he had with the A’s.  Lifetime, Holliday has a .354 BABIP.  He should be a good bet to once again finish with a high average.  However, it is unlikely Holliday will ever again match the 28 steals he posted in 2008.  Last year’s total of 14 was right in line with his numbers from 2005-07.  (Brian Joura)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Matt Holliday is one of those players on whom you can count nearly every season. He'll hit between 25 and 30 home runs, will drive in 100 or more, will score around 100, and will hit .300. While there are no guarantees, Holliday presents as good a bet as any in the outfield. He might even benefit more, by way of RBI, if the Cardinals can fit in some better hitters at the top of the order. Even if they don't, they'll always have Pujols hitting in front of him. In addition to the consistent performance, there's always the chance Holliday has a 2007-esque season. That makes him a safe bet with potential to be a top-five performer. He likely won't make it out of most first rounds.  (Joe Pawlikowski)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Consistent, predictable performers are fantasy heaven, and that is Matt Holliday embodied. Count on .300-plus with around 100 runs scored, 100 RBI, and 25 to 30 HR. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Holliday didn’t put up the bulk numbers that he is used to thanks to a variety of injuries, but his rate stats all remained intact. In fact, Big Daddy’s 154 wRC+ was the best mark of his career, and among outfielders with at least 500 plate appearances, it was bested only by Jose Bautista, Ryan Braun, Matt Kemp and teammate Lance Berkman. Holliday doesn’t run like he used to -- after swiping 28 bags in 30 tries during 2008 he has only attempted 38 in the three seasons since, including just three last year. Nevertheless, if Holliday can keep himself on the field, he will be one of the most valuable outfielders in the game, even without Albert Pujols hitting in front of him. (Paul Swydan)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Holliday missed some time last season, and the resulting drop in his bulk stats may leave him underrated heading into 2012. Don’t be fooled, he is still an elite hitter.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Matt Holliday is the Toyota Camry of outfielders. He’s not flashy, he won’t be one of the first chosen, but he’s reliably above average every single year. Fantasy owners can count on him to provide roughly 25 home runs, 100 RBI, 100 runs, and a .300 batting average. He’s no longer the stolen base threat he was in Colorado, but that’s splitting hairs. Holliday’s .378 wOBA ranked sixth among outfielders and his ISO was north of .200 for the seventh-consecutive season. Some have expressed concern about his .337 BABIP in 2012, but that actually checked in below his career-average .345 BABIP. Barring injury -- which could become a common conditional statement since this will be his age-33 season, and the loss of speed suggest he's already feeling the effect of age -- Holliday will produce as he always does. A potent Cardinals lineup will only help the RBI and run totals, too. (JP Breen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Matt Holliday remains one of the most consistent fantasy producers available, with only a lack of stolen bases preventing him from being a true first-tier outfielder.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Holliday continues to display a crazy degree of consistency at the plate, now having posted weighted on-base averages between .378 and .397 in each of the last five seasons. His power was down a bit, supported by a decline in his average home run and fly ball distance, but not enough so to sound the alarm bells. He'll be entering his age-34 season, though, so age-related decline should start to rear its ugly head. That might mean the end of his smattering of stolen bases that added the cherry on top of his sundae of sweet production. Aside from the slight power dip, he has maintained strong skills with nothing in his metrics raising any red flags. (<a href="https://twitter.com/MikePodhorzer" target="_blank">Mike Podhorzer)</a><br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Holliday has been as consistent as it gets at the plate, providing four-category production plus a bonus of non-zero steals totals. While nothing in his metrics suggest an age-related collapse is imminent, it is worth remembering that he'll be 34 this upcoming season and that could mean some further decline is in the cards (pun very much intended).</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Time seemed to have finally caught up with the 34-year-old Matt Holliday in the first half of 2014, when he hit just .265 with six home runs. But, after a second-half surge, Holliday reached 20 home runs for the ninth consecutive season. His .272 batting average was 36 points down from his career average, but his .298 batting average on balls in play was 40 points below his career level and 24 points down from 2013. Soon to be 35 years old, Holliday could be on the brink of decline, but his 2014 season is not clear evidence that the decline has already begun. Instead, Holliday remains one of the most consistent players in baseball and should be considered a top 30 outfielder in 2015. (Scott Spratt)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A strong second half for Holliday in 2014 staved off the fears of age-related decline. He remains one of the most consistent players in baseball and is a top 30 outfielder for 2015.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Matt Holliday had been ridiculously consistent with his production before the 2015 season. He had his ninth straight 20-homer season in 2014 to go along with a decent .272 average and very good .370 on-base percentage. He has hit double-digits in walk-rate every year with the Cardinals, and has occupied the third spot in the batting order since Albert Pujols left for Anaheim. That 2014 season did show a bit of a decline, as his power and average both dropped a bit, not unusual for a player entering his mid-30s. That consistency came to halt last season. Holliday got off to a slow start in the power department, but the batting eye was still there as he walked in 14% of his plate appearances. He then injured his hamstring, rushed his return, re-injured the hamstring and missed the bulk of the season. Holliday has never struck out much, and always had a good eye which should keep his batting average and on-base percentage above average. His power might not recover to even 2014 levels so banking on 20 homers is a stretch. At 36, Holliday should still be a good player, especially for his age, but the fears that began to surface in 2014 were evident last season, and expecting past performance to come back at his age is unlikely. (Craig Edwards)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Matt Holliday is no longer the offensive force he once was, but he still has a good eye and hits the ball hard. Expect a decent batting average, a solid on-base percentage and a good spot in the lineup for a player who probably has just a little bit of pop left.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Matt Holliday has spent the bulk of his 30s mashing with the Cardinals, but after two injury-shortened seasons, he will head to the Yankees, presumably to serve as the team's everyday designated hitter. He still managed to hit 20 home runs last season in just over 400 plate appearances, and his .253 batting average on balls in play is an indicator that he had some bad luck sink his batting average, which was a career low .246 last year. He's always had a good eye, generally displaying a double-digit walk rate and better-than-average strikeout rates, especially for a guy with his power. Seeing Holliday hit another 20 homers wouldn't come as a shock. Busch Stadium has long been one of the more difficult parks for right-handed hitters to hit homers, and while Yankees Stadium is more favorable to lefties, it's still a good homer park for righties, and Holliday does have power to all fields. All that said, Holliday is now 37 years old and hasn't stayed healthy the last two seasons. There's some power and counting stats upside there if you are taking a flier, but counting on anything resembling his past numbers is too big a risk to take. (Craig Edwards)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Matt Holliday might well hit more than 20 homers and come close to 100 RBI, but there's considerable risk that age catches up to him and even more modest numbers will be difficult to attain.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
